missions">Horizon 2000+</a></b></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/LISA_Pathfinder" title="LISA Pathfinder">LISA Pathfinder</a></i></div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>BepiColombo</b></i> is a joint mission of the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) and the <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency</a> (JAXA) to the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amos_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amos-4">[4]</a></sup> The mission comprises two satellites launched together: the <b>Mercury Planetary Orbiter</b> (<b>MPO</b>) and <i><b>Mio</b></i> (<b>Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter</b>, <b>MMO</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miopressrelease_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miopressrelease-5">[5]</a></sup> The mission will perform a comprehensive study of Mercury, including characterization of its <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, and both interior and surface structure. It was launched on an <a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ariane_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ariane-2">[2]</a></sup> rocket on 20 October 2018 at 01:45 UTC, with an arrival at Mercury planned for December 2025, after a flyby of Earth, two flybys of Venus, and six flybys of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20161125_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20161125-6">[6]</a></sup> The mission was approved in November 2009, after years in proposal and planning as part of the European Space Agency\'s <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_Space_Agency_programs_and_missions#Horizon_2000+" title="List of European Space Agency programs and missions">Horizon 2000+</a> programme;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> it is the last mission of the programme to be launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scaling_back_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-scaling_back-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" tocsection-11"><a href="#Science_payload_2"><span class="tocnumber">2.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Science payload</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Mercury_Surface_Element_(cancelled)"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Mercury Surface Element (cancelled)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission">Mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>BepiColombo</i> is named after <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Colombo" title="Giuseppe Colombo">Giuseppe "Bepi" Colombo</a> (1920&#8211;1984), a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a>, Italy, who first implemented the interplanetary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity-assist" title="Gravity-assist">gravity-assist</a> manoeuvre during the 1974 <i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a></i> mission, a technique now commonly used by planetary probes.\n</p><p>The mission involves three components, which will separate into independent spacecraft upon arrival at Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Mercury Transfer Module (MTM) for propulsion, built by ESA</li>\n<li>Mercury Planetary Orbiter (MPO) built by ESA</li>\n<li>Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter (MMO) or <i>Mio</i> built by JAXA</li></ul>\n<p>During launch and cruise phase, these three components are joined together to form the Mercury Cruise System (MCS).\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_contractor" title="Prime contractor">prime contractor</a> for ESA is <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Defence_and_Space" title="Airbus Defence and Space">Airbus Defence and Space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci.esa.int_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci.esa.int-10">[10]</a></sup> ESA is responsible for the overall mission, the design, development assembly and test of the propulsion and MPO modules, and the launch. The two orbiters were successfully launched together on 20 October 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> on <a href="/wiki/Ariane_flight_VA245" title="Ariane flight VA245">Ariane flight VA245</a>. The spacecraft will have a seven-year interplanetary cruise to Mercury using solar-electric propulsion (<a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion thrusters</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assists</a> from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and eventual gravity capture at <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS-1">[1]</a></sup> ESA\'s <a href="/wiki/Cebreros_Station" title="Cebreros Station">Cebreros</a> 35-metre ground station is planned to be the primary ground facility for communications during all mission phases.\n</p><p>Arriving in Mercury orbit on 5 December 2025, the <i>Mio</i> and MPO satellites will separate and observe Mercury in collaboration for one year, with a possible one-year extension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS-1">[1]</a></sup>  The orbiters are equipped with scientific instruments provided by various European countries and Japan. The mission will characterize the solid and liquid iron <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> (&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub></span> of the planet\'s radius)  and determine the size of each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The mission will also complete <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> mappings. Russia provided <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_spectrometer" title="Gamma-ray spectrometer">gamma ray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_spectroscopy" title="Neutron spectroscopy">neutron spectrometers</a> to verify the existence of water ice in polar craters that are permanently in shadow from the Sun\'s rays.\n</p><p>Mercury is too small and hot for its gravity to retain any significant atmosphere over long periods of time, but it has a "tenuous surface-bounded <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">exosphere</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> containing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and other trace elements. Its exosphere is not stable as atoms are continuously lost and replenished from a variety of sources. The mission will study the exosphere composition and dynamics, including generation and escape.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The main objectives of the mission are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISAS_homepage_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISAS_homepage-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS2_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS2-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Study the origin and evolution of a planet close to its parent star</li>\n<li>Study Mercury\'s form, interior, structure, geology, composition and craters</li>\n<li>Investigate <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">Mercury\'s exosphere</a>, composition and dynamics, including generation and escape</li>\n<li>Study Mercury\'s magnetised envelope (<a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>) - structure and dynamics</li>\n<li>Investigate the origin of <a href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_magnetic_field" title="Mercury\'s magnetic field">Mercury\'s magnetic field</a></li>\n<li>Verify Einstein\'s theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> by measuring the parameters <i>gamma</i> and <i>beta</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Parameterized_post-Newtonian_formalism" title="Parameterized post-Newtonian formalism">parameterized post-Newtonian formalism</a> with high accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="1655" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg/220px-MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg/330px-MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg/440px-MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MMO%26MPO-Orbits.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Planned orbits for <i>Mio</i> and MPO satellites, the two probes of the <i>BepiColombo</i> mission</div></div></div>\n<p>The stacked spacecraft will take seven years to position itself to enter Mercury orbit. During this time it will use <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">solar-electric propulsion</a> and nine gravity assists, flying past the Earth and Moon in April 2020, Venus in 2020 and 2021, and six Mercury flybys between 2021 and 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The stacked spacecraft left Earth with a <a href="/wiki/Hyperbola" title="Hyperbola">hyperbolic</a> excess velocity of 3.475&#160;km/s (2.159&#160;mi/s). Initially the craft is placed in an orbit similar to that of the Earth. After both the spacecraft and the Earth completed one and a half orbits, it returns to Earth to perform a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity-assist" title="Gravity-assist">gravity-assist</a> manoeuvre and is deflected towards Venus. Two consecutive Venus flybys reduce the perihelion nearly to Mercury distance with almost no need for thrust. A sequence of six Mercury flybys will lower the relative velocity to 1.76&#160;km/s (1.09&#160;mi/s). After the fourth Mercury flyby the craft will be in an orbit similar to that of Mercury and will remain in the general vicinity of Mercury (see <a class="external text" href="http://sci.esa.int/bepicolombo/59288-bepicolombo-s-journey-to-mercury/" rel="nofollow">video</a>). Four final thrust arcs reduce the relative velocity to the point where Mercury will "weakly" capture the spacecraft on 5 December 2025 into polar orbit. Only a small manoeuvre is needed to bring the craft into an orbit around Mercury with an apocentre of 178,000&#160;km. The orbiters then separate and will adjust their orbits using chemical thrusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Data_Center_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Data_Center-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mission_design_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-mission_design-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schedule">Schedule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Schedule">edit</a><span planned mission schedule is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table align="left" border="1" cellpadding="5" cellspacing="0" Red circle indicates flybys.</div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>BepiColombo</i> mission proposal was approved in 2000 by the ESA, with a science payload proposal request issued in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-19">[19]</a></sup> In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Astrium" title="Astrium">Astrium</a> was selected as the prime contractor, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soyuz-Fregat" title="Soyuz-Fregat">Soyuz-Fregat</a>  launcher was dropped in favor of Ariane 5 as the estimated mass increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-19">[19]</a></sup> The initial target launch of July 2014 was postponed several times, mostly because of delays on the development of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_electric_propulsion" title="Solar electric propulsion">solar electric propulsion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-19">[19]</a></sup> The total cost of the mission is estimated at US$2 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_Transfer_Module">Mercury Transfer Module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mercury Transfer Module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3336" data-file-width="5008" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg/220px-Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg/330px-Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg/440px-Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Transfer_Module_in_space_simulator_ESA387743.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mercury Transfer Module in The Large Space Simulator at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESTEC" title="ESTEC">ESTEC</a>.</div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Qinetiq" title="Qinetiq">QinetiQ T6</a></th>\n<th>Performance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Type</td>\n<td>Kaufman Ion Engine\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Units on board</td>\n<td>4 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Diameter</td>\n<td>22 centimetres (8.7&#160;in)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Max. thrust</td>\n<td>145&#160;mN each\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">Specific impulse</a><br/>(I<sub>sp</sub>)</td>\n<td>4,300 s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Propellant</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">Xenon</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Total power</td>\n<td>4,628 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The Mercury Transfer Module (MTM) is located at the base of the stack. Its role is to carry the two science orbiters to Mercury and to support them during the cruise.\n</p><p>The MTM is equipped with a <a href="/wiki/Solar_electric_propulsion" title="Solar electric propulsion">solar electric propulsion</a> system as the main spacecraft propulsion. Its four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QinetiQ" title="QinetiQ">QinetiQ</a>&#160;T6 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_thrusters" title="Ion thrusters">ion thrusters</a> operate singly or in pair for a maximum combined thrust of 290&#160;mN,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> making it the most powerful ion engine ever operated in space. The MTM supplies electrical power for the two hibernating orbiters as well as for its solar electric propulsion system thanks to two 14-meter-long <a href="/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft" title="Solar panels on spacecraft">solar panels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPO_page_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPO_page-26">[26]</a></sup> Depending on the probe\'s distance to the Sun, the generated power will range between 7 and 14&#160;kW, each T6 requiring between 2.5 and 4.5&#160;kW according to the desired thrust level.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_electric_propulsion" title="Solar electric propulsion">solar electric propulsion</a> system has typically very high <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a> and low <a href="/wiki/Thrust" title="Thrust">thrust</a>. This leads to a flight profile with months-long continuous low-thrust braking phases, interrupted by planetary <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assists</a>, to gradually reduce the velocity of the spacecraft. Moments before Mercury orbit insertion, the MTM will be jettisoned from the spacecraft stack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPO_page_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-MPO_page-26">[26]</a></sup> After separation from the MTM, the MPO will provide <i>Mio</i> all necessary power and data resources until <i>Mio</i> is delivered to its mission orbit; separation of <i>Mio</i> from MPO will be accomplished by spin-ejection.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_Planetary_Orbiter">Mercury Planetary Orbiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mercury Planetary Orbiter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2065" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg/220px-BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg/330px-BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg/440px-BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BepiColombo_MPO_ESTEC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mercury Planetary Orbiter in ESTEC before stacking</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3066" data-file-width="4599" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg/220px-Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg/330px-Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg/440px-Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radio_testing_of_BepiColombo_orbiter_ESA353568.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Radio testing of <i>BepiColombo</i> orbiter</div></div></div>\n<p>The Mercury Planetary Orbiter (MPO) will have a mass of 1,150&#160;kg (2,540&#160;lb) and will use a single-sided solar array capable of providing up to 1000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> and featuring Optical Solar Reflectors to keep its temperature below 200&#160;&#176;C (392&#160;&#176;F). The solar array requires continuous rotation keeping the Sun at a low incidence angle in order to generate adequate power while at the same time limiting the temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPO_page_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-MPO_page-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The MPO will carry a payload of 11 instruments, comprising cameras, spectrometers (IR, UV, X-ray, &#947;-ray, neutron), a radiometer, a laser altimeter, a magnetometer, particle analysers, a K<sub>a</sub>-band transponder, and an accelerometer. The payload components are mounted on the nadir side of the spacecraft to achieve low detector temperatures, apart from the MERTIS and PHEBUS spectrometers located directly at the main radiator to provide a better field of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPO_page_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-MPO_page-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A high-temperature-resistant 1.0&#160;m (3.3&#160;ft) diameter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a> is mounted on a short boom on the zenith side of the spacecraft.  Communications will be on the <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ka_band" title="Ka band">K<sub>a</sub> band</a> with an average bit rate of 50&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_rate_units#Kilobit_per_second" title="Data rate units">kbit/s</a> and a total data volume of 1550&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gigabit" title="Gigabit">Gbit</a>/year. ESA\'s <a href="/wiki/Cebreros_Station" title="Cebreros Station">Cebreros</a> 35-metre ground station is planned to be the primary ground facility for communications during all mission phases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPO_page_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-MPO_page-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_payload">Science payload</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Science payload">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The science payload of the Mercury Planetary Orbiter consists of eleven instruments:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMO_objectives_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMO_objectives-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPO_Instruments_2008_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPO_Instruments_2008-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>BepiColombo <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">Laser Altimeter</a> (BELA), developed by Switzerland and Germany</li>\n<li>Italian Spring <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">Accelerometer</a> (ISA), developed by Italy</li>\n<li>Mercury <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a> (MPO-MAG, MERMAG), developed by Germany and UK<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Mercury Radiometer and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared_spectroscopy" title="Thermal infrared spectroscopy">Thermal Infrared Spectrometer</a> (MERTIS), developed by Germany</li>\n<li>Mercury <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_spectrometer#Planetary_gamma-ray_spectrometers" title="Gamma-ray spectrometer">Gamma-ray and Neutron Spectrometer</a> (MGNS), developed by Russia</li>\n<li>Mercury <a href="/wiki/Phase-contrast_X-ray_imaging" title="Phase-contrast X-ray imaging">Imaging X-ray Spectrometer</a> (MIXS), developed by UK and Finland</li>\n<li>Mercury Orbiter <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_radio_science" title="Outline of radio science">Radio-science</a> Experiment (MORE), developed by Italy and the United States</li>\n<li>Probing of Hermean <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">Exosphere</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet%E2%80%93visible_spectroscopy" title="Ultraviolet&#8211;visible spectroscopy">Ultraviolet Spectroscopy</a> (PHEBUS), developed by France and Russia</li>\n<li>Search for Exosphere Refilling and Emitted Neutral Abundances (SERENA), a neutral and ionised particle analyser, developed by Italy, Sweden, Austria and the United States, which contains the <i>Strofio</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">mass spectrometer</a> from NASA\'s Discovery programme<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Spectrometers and Imagers for MPO BepiColombo Integrated Observatory System (SIMBIO-SYS), high resolution stereo cameras and a visual and <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">near infrared spectrometer</a>, developed by Italy, France and Switzerland</li>\n<li>Solar Intensity <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle_X-ray_spectrometer" title="Alpha particle X-ray spectrometer">X-ray and Particle Spectrometer</a> (SIXS), developed by Finland and UK</li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Mio_.28Mercury_Magnetospheric_Orbiter.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mio_(Mercury_Magnetospheric_Orbiter)"><i>Mio</i> (Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mio (Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1036" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg/220px-BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg/330px-BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg/440px-BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BepiColombo_MMO_ESTEC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Mio</i> in ESTEC before stacking</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Mio</i>, or the Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter (MMO), developed and built mostly by <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, has the shape of a short octagonal prism, 180&#160;cm (71&#160;in) long from face to face and 90&#160;cm (35&#160;in) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISAS_homepage_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-ISAS_homepage-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It has a mass of 285&#160;kg (628&#160;lb), including a 45&#160;kg (99&#160;lb) scientific payload consisting of 5 instrument groups, 4 for plasma and dust measuring run by investigators from Japan, and one <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a> from Austria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISAS_homepage_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-ISAS_homepage-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMO_brochure_2015_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMO_brochure_2015-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Mio</i> is spin stabilized at 15 rpm with the spin axis perpendicular to the equator of Mercury and it will enter polar orbit at an altitude of 590&#160;&#215;&#160;11,640&#160;km (370&#160;&#215;&#160;7,230&#160;mi), outside of MPO\'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMO_brochure_2015_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMO_brochure_2015-32">[32]</a></sup> The top and bottom of the octagon act as radiators with louvers for active temperature control. The sides are covered with <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> which provide 90&#160;W. Communications with Earth will be through a 0.8&#160;m (2.6&#160;ft) diameter <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a> phased array <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a> and two medium-gain antennas operating in the X&#160;band. Telemetry will return 160&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gigabit" title="Gigabit">Gb</a>/year, about 5&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kbit/s#Kilobit_per_second" title="Kbit/s">kbit/s</a> over the lifetime of the spacecraft, which is expected to be greater than one year. The reaction and control system is based on <a href="/wiki/Cold_gas_thruster" title="Cold gas thruster">cold gas thrusters</a>. After its release in Mercury orbit, <i>Mio</i> will be operated by Sagamihara Space Operation Center using <a href="/wiki/Usuda_Deep_Space_Center" title="Usuda Deep Space Center">Usuda Deep Space Center</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> 64&#160;m (210&#160;ft) antenna located in <a href="/wiki/Nagano_Prefecture" title="Nagano Prefecture">Nagano, Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMO_objectives_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMO_objectives-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter was given the nickname <i>Mio</i> on 8 June 2018. In Japanese, <i>Mio</i> means a waterway for ships, and symbolizes the research and development milestones reached thus far, as well as wishes for a safe travel ahead. The spacecraft will explore the solar wind as it flows through and is interfered by Mercury\'s magnetosphere, just like a ship voyaging through water currents. <i>Mio</i> was chosen from among 6,494 submissions from the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miopressrelease_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miopressrelease-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_payload_2">Science payload</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Science payload">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><i>Mio</i> carries five groups of science instruments with a total mass of 45&#160;kg (99&#160;lb):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISAS_homepage_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-ISAS_homepage-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMO_objectives_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMO_objectives-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Mercury Plasma Particle Experiment (MPPE), studies the plasma &amp; neutral particles from the planet, magnetosphere, and interplanetary <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>. It will employ these instruments:\n<ul><li>Mercury Electron Analyzers (MEA1 and MEA2)</li>\n<li>Mercury Ion Analyzer (MIA)</li>\n<li>Mass Spectrum Analyzer (MSA)</li>\n<li>High-Energy Particle instrument for electrons (HEP-ele)</li>\n<li>High-Energy Particle instrument for Ions (HEP-ion)</li>\n<li>Energetic Neutrals Analyzer (ENA)</li></ul></li>\n<li>Mercury <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a> (MMO-MGF), studies <a href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_magnetic_field" title="Mercury\'s magnetic field">Mercury\'s magnetic field</a>, magnetosphere, and interplanetary <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a></li>\n<li>Plasma Wave Investigation (PWI), studies the electric field, electromagnetic waves, and radio waves from the magnetosphere and solar wind</li>\n<li>Mercury Sodium Atmosphere Spectral Imager (MSASI), studies the thin sodium <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">atmosphere of Mercury</a></li>\n<li>Mercury Dust Monitor (MDM), studies dust from the planet and interplanetary space</li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Mercury_Surface_Element_.28cancelled.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_Surface_Element_(cancelled)">Mercury Surface Element (cancelled)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mercury Surface Element (cancelled)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Mercury Surface Element (MSE) was cancelled in 2003 due to budgetary constraints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scaling_back_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-scaling_back-8">[8]</a></sup> At the time of cancellation, MSE was meant to be a small, 44&#160;kg (97&#160;lb), lander designed to operate for about one week on the surface of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Data_Center_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Data_Center-17">[17]</a></sup> Shaped as a 0.9&#160;m (3.0&#160;ft) diameter disc, it was designed to land at a latitude of 85&#176; near the terminator region. Braking manoeuvres would bring the lander to zero velocity at an altitude of 120&#160;m (390&#160;ft) at which point the propulsion unit would be ejected, the airbags inflated, and the module would fall to the surface with a maximum impact velocity of 30&#160;m/s (98&#160;ft/s). Scientific data would be stored onboard and relayed via a cross-dipole <a href="/wiki/Ultra_high_frequency" title="Ultra high frequency">UHF</a> antenna to either the MPO or <i>Mio</i>. The MSE would have carried a 7&#160;kg (15&#160;lb) payload consisting of an imaging system (a descent camera and a surface camera), a heat flow and physical properties package, an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle_X-ray_spectrometer" title="Alpha particle X-ray spectrometer">alpha particle X-ray spectrometer</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">seismometer</a>, a soil penetrating device (mole), and a micro-<a href="/wiki/Rover_(space_exploration)" title="Rover (space exploration)">rover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mercury" title="Exploration of Mercury">Exploration of Mercury</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i> &#8211;  the first spacecraft to orbit Mercury</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=BepiColombo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-FACTS-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FACTS_1-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.esa.int/Our_Activities/Space_Science/BepiColombo/BepiColombo_Factsheet" rel="nofollow">"BepiColombo Factsheet"</a>. <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>. 6 July 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">.
